For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Petroleum_(disambiguation)" title="Petroleum (disambiguation)">Petroleum (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

href="/wiki/File:Photo_lg_kuwait.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="398" data-file-width="599" decoding="async" height="146" class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mina_Al_Ahmadi" title="Mina Al Ahmadi">Mina Al Ahmadi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p><b>Petroleum</b>, also known as <b>crude oil</b>, or simply <b>oil</b>, is a naturally occurring yellowish-black <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a> mixture of mainly <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> and is found in <a href="/wiki/Geological_formation" title="Geological formation">geological formations</a>. The name <i>petroleum</i> covers both naturally occurring unprocessed crude oil and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_product" title="Petroleum product">petroleum products</a> that consist of refined crude oil. A <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>, petroleum is formed when large quantities of dead organisms, mostly <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, are buried underneath <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a> and subjected to both prolonged heat and pressure.
</p><p>Petroleum has mostly been recovered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_drilling" title="Oil drilling">oil drilling</a>. Drilling is carried out after studies of structural geology, sedimentary basin analysis, and reservoir characterisation. Recent developments in technologies have also led to exploitation of other unconventional reserves such as <a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">oil sands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">oil shale</a>. Once extracted, oil is refined and separated, most easily by <a href="/wiki/Continuous_distillation#Continuous_distillation_of_crude_oil" title="Continuous distillation">distillation</a>, into numerous products for direct use or use in manufacturing. Products include fuels such as <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> (petrol), <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>; <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a>; and chemical <a href="/wiki/Reagent" title="Reagent">reagents</a> used to make <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceuticals" title="Pharmaceuticals">pharmaceuticals</a>. Petroleum is used in manufacturing a wide variety of materials,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dixie_State_College_2_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dixie_State_College_2-2">[2]</a></sup> and it is estimated that the world consumes about 100 million <a href="/wiki/Barrel_(unit)#Oil_barrel" title="Barrel (unit)">barrels</a> (16 million <a href="/wiki/Cubic_metre" title="Cubic metre">cubic metres</a>) each day. Petroleum production can be extremely profitable and was important for economic development in the 20th century, with some countries, so called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_states" title="Oil states">oil states</a>", gaining significant economic and international power because of their control of oil production.
</p><p>Petroleum exploitation has significant negative environmental and social consequences. Most significantly, <a href="/wiki/Extraction_of_petroleum" title="Extraction of petroleum">extraction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">refining</a> and <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">burning</a> of petroleum fuels all release large quantities of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>, so petroleum is one of the <a href="/wiki/Attribution_of_recent_climate_change" title="Attribution of recent climate change">major contributors to climate change</a>. Furthermore, parts of the <a href="/wiki/ExxonMobil_climate_change_controversy" title="ExxonMobil climate change controversy">petroleum industry actively suppressed science and policy</a> that aimed to prevent the <a href="/wiki/Climate_crisis" title="Climate crisis">climate crisis</a>. Other <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_petroleum_industry" title="Environmental impact of the petroleum industry">negative environmental effects</a> include the environmental impacts of exploration and exploitation of petroleum reserves, such as <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">oil spills</a>, and air and water pollution at the sites of utilization. All of these environmental impacts have direct health consequences for humans. Additionally, oil has also been a source of conflict leading to both <a href="/wiki/Oil_war" title="Oil war">state-led-wars</a> and other kinds of conflicts (for example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_production_and_smuggling_in_ISIL" title="Oil production and smuggling in ISIL">oil revenue funded</a> the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State" title="Islamic State">Islamic State</a>).  Production of petroleum is expected to reach <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a> before 2035<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> as global economies lower dependencies on petroleum as part of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">climate change mitigation</a> and a transition towards <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrification" title="Electrification">electrification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> This is expected to have significant economic impacts that stakeholders argue need to be anticipated by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Just_Transition" title="Just Transition">just transition</a> and addressing the <a href="/wiki/Stranded_asset" title="Stranded asset">stranded assets</a> of the petroleum industry.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2892" data-file-width="3856" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg/220px-Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg/330px-Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg/440px-Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fractional_distillation_apparatus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fractional distillation apparatus.</div></div></div>
<p>The word <i>petroleum</i> comes from Medieval Latin <i lang="la"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/petroleum#Latin" title="wikt:petroleum">petroleum</a></i> (literally 'rock oil'), which comes from Latin <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/petra#Latin" title="wikt:petra">petra</a> 'rock' (from Greek <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">pétra</i></span> <span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%80%CE%AD%CF%84%CF%81%CE%B1#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:πέτρα">πέτρα</a></span>) and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/oleum#Latin" title="wikt:oleum">oleum</a> 'oil' (from Greek <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">élaion</i></span> <span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%94%CE%BB%CE%B1%CE%B9%CE%BF%CE%BD#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:ἔλαιον">ἔλαιον</a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term was used in the treatise <i><a href="/wiki/De_Natura_Fossilium" title="De Natura Fossilium">De Natura Fossilium</a></i>, published in 1546 by the German mineralogist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georg_Bauer" title="Georg Bauer">Georg Bauer</a>, also known as Georgius Agricola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In the 19th century, the term <i>petroleum</i> was often used to refer to <a href="/wiki/Mineral_oil" title="Mineral oil">mineral oils</a> produced by distillation from mined organic solids such as <a href="/wiki/Cannel_coal" title="Cannel coal">cannel coal</a> (and later <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">oil shale</a>) and refined oils produced from them; in the United Kingdom, storage (and later transport) of these oils were regulated by a series of Petroleum Acts, from the <i>Petroleum Act 1863</i> onwards.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_petroleum_industry" title="History of the petroleum industry">History of the petroleum industry</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early">Early</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="956" data-file-width="654" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg/170px-Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg/255px-Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg/340px-Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gusher_Okemah_OK_1922.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil derrick in <a href="/wiki/Okemah,_Oklahoma" title="Okemah, Oklahoma">Okemah, Oklahoma</a>, 1922.</div></div></div>
<p>Petroleum, in one form or another, has been used since ancient times, and is now important across society, including in economy, politics and technology. The rise in importance was due to the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a>, the rise in <a href="/wiki/Commercial_aviation" title="Commercial aviation">commercial aviation</a>, and the importance of petroleum to industrial organic chemistry, particularly the synthesis of plastics, fertilisers, solvents, adhesives and pesticides.
</p><p>Before more than 4300 years ago, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a> was mentioned when the Sumerians used it to make boats. Tablet of the legend of the birth of Sargon of Akkad, mentioned a basket which was closed by straw and bitumen. More than 4000 years ago, according to <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a> was used in the construction of the walls and towers of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>; there were oil pits near Ardericca (near Babylon), and a pitch spring on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zacynthus" title="Zacynthus">Zacynthus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-8">[8]</a></sup> Great quantities of it were found on the banks of the river <a href="/wiki/Issus_(river)" title="Issus (river)">Issus</a>, one of the tributaries of the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>. Ancient <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian</a> <a href="/wiki/Persepolis_Administrative_Archives" title="Persepolis Administrative Archives">tablets</a> indicate the medicinal and lighting uses of petroleum in the upper levels of their society.
</p><p>The use of petroleum in ancient China dates back to more than 2000 years ago. The <i><a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a></i>, one of the earliest Chinese writings, cites that oil in its raw state, without refining, was first discovered, extracted, and used in China in the first century BCE. In addition, the Chinese were the first to record the use of petroleum as fuel as early as the fourth century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> By 347 CE, oil was produced from bamboo-drilled wells in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">Crude oil</a> was often distilled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alchemy_and_chemistry_in_medieval_Islam" title="Alchemy and chemistry in medieval Islam">Persian chemists</a>, with clear descriptions given in Arabic handbooks such as those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakar%C4%ABya_R%C4%81zi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakarīya Rāzi">Muhammad ibn Zakarīya Rāzi</a> (Rhazes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The streets of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> were paved with <a href="/wiki/Tar" title="Tar">tar</a>, derived from petroleum that became accessible from natural fields in the region. In the 9th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil fields</a> were exploited in the area around modern <a href="/wiki/Baku" title="Baku">Baku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>. These fields were described by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_geography" title="Islamic geography">Arab geographer</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_al-Hasan_%27Al%C4%AB_al-Mas%27%C5%ABd%C4%AB" title="Abu al-Hasan 'Alī al-Mas'ūdī">Abu al-Hasan 'Alī al-Mas'ūdī</a> in the 10th century, and by <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> in the 13th century, who described the output of those wells as hundreds of shiploads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alchemy_and_chemistry_in_Islam" title="Alchemy and chemistry in Islam">Arab and Persian chemists</a> also distilled crude oil in order to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flammability" title="Flammability">flammable</a> products for military purposes. Through <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Islamic Spain</a>, distillation became available in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> by the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It has also been present in Romania since the 13th century, being recorded as păcură.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sophisticated oil pits, 4.5 to 6 metres (15 to 20 ft) deep, were dug by the <a href="/wiki/Seneca_people" title="Seneca people">Seneca People</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">Iroquois</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Pennsylvania" title="Western Pennsylvania">Western Pennsylvania</a> as early as 1415–1450. The French General <a href="/wiki/Louis-Joseph_de_Montcalm" title="Louis-Joseph de Montcalm">Louis-Joseph de Montcalm</a> encountered Seneca using petroleum for ceremonial fires and as a healing lotion during a visit to Fort Duquesne in 1750.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early British explorers to <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> documented a flourishing oil extraction industry based in <a href="/wiki/Yenangyaung" title="Yenangyaung">Yenangyaung</a> that, in 1795, had hundreds of hand-dug wells under production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Merkwiller-Pechelbronn" title="Merkwiller-Pechelbronn">Pechelbronn</a> (Pitch fountain) is said to be the first European site where petroleum has been explored and used. The still active Erdpechquelle, a spring where petroleum appears mixed with water has been used since 1498, notably for medical purposes. Oil sands have been mined since the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Wietze" title="Wietze">Wietze</a> in lower Saxony, natural asphalt/bitumen has been explored since the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Both in Pechelbronn as in Wietze, the coal industry dominated the petroleum technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chemist <a href="/wiki/James_Young_(chemist)" title="James Young (chemist)">James Young</a> noticed a natural petroleum seepage in the <a href="/wiki/Riddings" title="Riddings">Riddings</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colliery" title="Colliery">colliery</a> at <a href="/wiki/Alfreton" title="Alfreton">Alfreton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire" title="Derbyshire">Derbyshire</a> from which he distilled a light thin oil suitable for use as lamp oil, at the same time obtaining a more viscous oil suitable for lubricating machinery. In 1848, Young set up a small business refining the crude oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-russell-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Young eventually succeeded, by distilling <a href="/wiki/Cannel_coal" title="Cannel coal">cannel coal</a> at a low heat, in creating a fluid resembling petroleum, which when treated in the same way as the seep oil gave similar products. Young found that by slow distillation he could obtain a number of useful liquids from it, one of which he named "paraffine oil" because at low temperatures it congealed into a substance resembling paraffin wax.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-russell-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The production of these oils and solid <a href="/wiki/Paraffin_wax" title="Paraffin wax">paraffin wax</a> from coal formed the subject of his patent dated 17 October 1850. In 1850 Young &amp; Meldrum and Edward William Binney entered into partnership under the title of E.W. Binney &amp; Co. at <a href="/wiki/Bathgate" title="Bathgate">Bathgate</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Lothian" title="West Lothian">West Lothian</a> and E. Meldrum &amp; Co. at Glasgow; their works at Bathgate were completed in 1851 and became the first truly commercial oil-works in the world with the first modern oil refinery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:West_Lothian_shale_bing,_Scotland.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG/220px-West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG/330px-West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG/440px-West_Lothian_shale_bing%2C_Scotland.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:West_Lothian_shale_bing,_Scotland.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shale bings near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broxburn,_West_Lothian" title="Broxburn, West Lothian">Broxburn</a>, 3 of a total of 19 in <a href="/wiki/West_Lothian" title="West Lothian">West Lothian</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The world's first oil refinery was built in 1856 by <a href="/wiki/Ignacy_%C5%81ukasiewicz" title="Ignacy Łukasiewicz">Ignacy Łukasiewicz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> His achievements also included the discovery of how to distill kerosene from seep oil, the invention of the modern kerosene lamp (1853), the introduction of the first modern street lamp in Europe (1853), and the construction of the world's first modern oil well (1854).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The demand for petroleum as a fuel for lighting in North America and around the world quickly grew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Drake" title="Edwin Drake">Edwin Drake</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Drake_Well" title="Drake Well">1859 well</a> near Titusville, Pennsylvania, is popularly considered the first modern well. Already 1858 Georg Christian Konrad Hunäus had found a significant amount of petroleum while drilling for <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">lignite</a> 1858 in <a href="/wiki/Wietze" title="Wietze">Wietze</a>, Germany. Wietze later provided about 80% of the German consumption in the Wilhelminian Era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The production stopped in 1963, but Wietze has hosted a Petroleum Museum since 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Drake's well is probably singled out because it was drilled, not dug; because it used a steam engine; because there was a company associated with it; and because it touched off a major boom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> However, there was considerable activity before Drake in various parts of the world in the mid-19th century. A group directed by Major Alexeyev of the Bakinskii Corps of Mining Engineers hand-drilled a well in the Baku region of Bibi-Heybat in 1846.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> There were engine-drilled wells in West Virginia in the same year as Drake's well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> An early commercial well was hand dug in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> in 1853, and another in nearby <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> in 1857. At around the same time the world's first, small, oil refinery was opened at <a href="/wiki/Jas%C5%82o" title="Jasło">Jasło</a> in Poland, with a larger one opened at <a href="/wiki/Ploie%C8%99ti" title="Ploiești">Ploiești</a> in Romania shortly after. Romania is the first country in the world to have had its annual crude oil output officially recorded in international statistics: 275 tonnes for 1857.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_petroleum_industry_in_Canada#Early_origins" title="History of the petroleum industry in Canada">first commercial oil well</a> in Canada became operational in 1858 at <a href="/wiki/Oil_Springs,_Ontario" title="Oil Springs, Ontario">Oil Springs, Ontario</a> (then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canada_West" title="Canada West">Canada West</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lclmg.org_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-lclmg.org-35">[35]</a></sup> Businessman <a href="/wiki/James_Miller_Williams" title="James Miller Williams">James Miller Williams</a> dug several wells between 1855 and 1858 before discovering a rich reserve of oil four metres below ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Unlear citation style. (December 2018)">specify</span></a></i>]</sup> Williams extracted 1.5 million litres of crude oil by 1860, refining much of it into kerosene lamp oil. Williams's well became commercially viable a year before Drake's Pennsylvania operation and could be argued to be the first commercial oil well in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The discovery at Oil Springs touched off an <a href="/wiki/Oil_boom" title="Oil boom">oil boom</a> which brought hundreds of speculators and workers to the area. Advances in drilling continued into 1862 when local driller Shaw reached a depth of 62 metres using the spring-pole drilling method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> On January 16, 1862, after an explosion of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, Canada's first oil gusher came into production, shooting into the air at a recorded rate of 480 cubic metres (3,000 bbl) per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> By the end of the 19th century the Russian Empire, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Branobel" title="Branobel">Branobel</a> company in <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, had taken the lead in production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Akiner_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Akiner-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ride_with_hitler.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="464" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ride_with_hitler.jpg/170px-Ride_with_hitler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ride_with_hitler.jpg/255px-Ride_with_hitler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ride_with_hitler.jpg/340px-Ride_with_hitler.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ride_with_hitler.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This wartime propaganda poster promoted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpooling" title="Carpooling">carpooling</a> as a way to ration vital gasoline during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Access to oil was and still is a major factor in several military conflicts of the twentieth century, including <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, during which oil facilities were a major strategic asset and were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_Campaign_chronology_of_World_War_II" title="Oil Campaign chronology of World War II">extensively bombed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">German invasion of the Soviet Union</a> included the goal to capture the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry_in_Azerbaijan" title="Petroleum industry in Azerbaijan">Baku oilfields</a>, as it would provide much needed oil-supplies for the German military which was suffering from blockades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Oil exploration in North America during the early 20th century later led to the US's becoming the leading producer by mid-century. As petroleum production in the US peaked during the 1960s, however, the United States was surpassed by Saudi Arabia and the Soviet Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973</a>, Saudi Arabia and other <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_Arab_Petroleum_Exporting_Countries" title="Organization of Arab Petroleum Exporting Countries">Arab nations</a> imposed an <a href="/wiki/Oil_embargo" title="Oil embargo">oil embargo</a> against the United States, United Kingdom, Japan and other Western nations which supported <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">Yom Kippur War</a> of October 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The embargo caused an <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">oil crisis</a>. This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/1979_oil_crisis" title="1979 oil crisis">1979 oil crisis</a>, which was caused by a drop in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_output" title="Oil output">oil production</a> in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">Iranian Revolution</a> and caused oil prices to more than double. The two oil price shocks had many short- and long-term effects on global politics and the global economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>  In particular, they led to sustained reductions in demand as a result of substitution to other fuels (especially coal and nuclear) and improvements in energy efficiency, facilitated by government policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> High oil prices also induced investment in oil production by non-OPEC countries, including Prudhoe Bay in Alaska, the North Sea offshore fields of the United Kingdom and Norway, the Cantarell offshore field of Mexico, and oil sands in Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, about 90 percent of vehicular fuel needs are met by oil. Petroleum also makes up 40 percent of total energy consumption in the United States, but is responsible for only 1 percent of electricity generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Petroleum's worth as a portable, dense energy source powering the vast majority of vehicles and as the base of many industrial chemicals makes it one of the world's most important <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a>.
</p><p>The top three oil producing countries are <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> In 2018, due in part to developments in <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Directional_drilling" title="Directional drilling">horizontal drilling</a>, the United States became the world's largest producer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._Energy_Information_Administration_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._Energy_Information_Administration-53">[53]</a></sup>
About 80 percent of the world's readily accessible reserves are located in the Middle East, with 62.5 percent coming from the Arab 5: <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>. A large portion of the world's total oil exists as unconventional sources, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_oil_sands" title="Athabasca oil sands">Athabasca oil sands</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra_heavy_oil" title="Extra heavy oil">extra heavy oil</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Orinoco_Belt" title="Orinoco Belt">Orinoco Belt</a>. While significant volumes of oil are extracted from oil sands, particularly in Canada, logistical and technical hurdles remain, as oil extraction requires large amounts of heat and water, making its net energy content quite low relative to conventional crude oil. Thus, Canada's oil sands are not expected to provide more than a few million barrels per day in the foreseeable future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Petroleum includes not only crude oil, but all liquid, gaseous and solid <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>. Under surface <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">pressure and temperature conditions</a>, lighter hydrocarbons <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a> exist as gases, while <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a> and heavier hydrocarbons are in the form of liquids or solids. However, in an underground <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_reservoir" title="Oil reservoir">oil reservoir</a> the proportions of gas, liquid, and solid depend on subsurface conditions and on the <a href="/wiki/Phase_diagram" title="Phase diagram">phase diagram</a> of the petroleum mixture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Oil_well" title="Oil well">oil well</a> produces predominantly crude oil, with some natural gas <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">dissolved</a> in it. Because the pressure is lower at the surface than underground, some of the gas will come out of <a href="/wiki/Solution_(chemistry)" title="Solution (chemistry)">solution</a> and be recovered (or burned) as <i>associated gas</i> or <i>solution gas</i>. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_well" title="Gas well">gas well</a> produces predominantly <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>. However, because the underground temperature is higher than at the surface, the gas may contain heavier hydrocarbons such as pentane, <a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heptane" title="Heptane">heptane</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaseous_state" title="Gaseous state">gaseous state</a>. At surface conditions these will <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condense</a> out of the gas to form "<a href="/wiki/Natural-gas_condensate" title="Natural-gas condensate">natural-gas condensate</a>", often shortened to <i>condensate.</i> Condensate resembles gasoline in appearance and is similar in composition to some <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a> <a href="/wiki/Light_crude_oil" title="Light crude oil">light crude oils</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The proportion of light hydrocarbons in the petroleum mixture varies greatly among different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_fields" title="Oil fields">oil fields</a>, ranging from as much as 97 percent by weight in the lighter oils to as little as 50 percent in the heavier oils and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumens</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The hydrocarbons in crude oil are mostly <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cycloalkane" title="Cycloalkane">cycloalkanes</a> and various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">aromatic hydrocarbons</a>, while the other organic compounds contain <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, and trace amounts of metals such as iron, nickel, copper and <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a>. Many oil reservoirs contain live bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The exact molecular composition of crude oil varies widely from formation to formation but the proportion of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> varies over fairly narrow limits as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<p>Four different types of hydrocarbon molecules appear in crude oil. The relative percentage of each varies from oil to oil, determining the properties of each oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="721" data-file-width="1281" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png/220px-Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png/330px-Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png/440px-Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Total_World_Oil_Reserves_Conventional_Unconventional.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unconventional resources are much larger than conventional ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Crude oil varies greatly in appearance depending on its composition. It is usually black or dark brown (although it may be yellowish, reddish, or even greenish). In the reservoir it is usually found in association with natural gas, which being lighter forms a "gas cap" over the petroleum, and <a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">saline water</a> which, being heavier than most forms of crude oil, generally sinks beneath it. Crude oil may also be found in a semi-solid form mixed with sand and water, as in the <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_oil_sands" title="Athabasca oil sands">Athabasca oil sands</a> in Canada, where it is usually referred to as crude <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a>. In Canada, bitumen is considered a sticky, black, tar-like form of crude oil which is so thick and heavy that it must be heated or diluted before it will flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Venezuela also has large amounts of oil in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orinoco_oil_sands" title="Orinoco oil sands">Orinoco oil sands</a>, although the hydrocarbons trapped in them are more fluid than in Canada and are usually called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra_heavy_oil" title="Extra heavy oil">extra heavy oil</a>. These oil sands resources are called <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_oil" title="Unconventional oil">unconventional oil</a> to distinguish them from oil which can be extracted using traditional oil well methods. Between them, Canada and <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> contain an estimated 3.6 trillion barrels (570<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup> m<sup>3</sup>) of bitumen and extra-heavy oil, about twice the volume of the world's reserves of conventional oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Petroleum is used mostly, by volume, for refining into <a href="/wiki/Fuel_oil" title="Fuel oil">fuel oil</a> and gasoline, both important <i>"<a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a>"</i> sources. 84 percent by volume of the hydrocarbons present in petroleum is converted into energy-rich fuels (petroleum-based fuels), including gasoline, diesel, jet, heating, and other fuel oils, and <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The lighter grades of crude oil produce the best yields of these products, but as the world's reserves of light and medium oil are depleted, <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refineries</a> are increasingly having to process heavy oil and bitumen, and use more complex and expensive methods to produce the products required. Because heavier crude oils have too much carbon and not enough hydrogen, these processes generally involve removing carbon from or adding hydrogen to the molecules, and using <a href="/wiki/Fluid_catalytic_cracking" title="Fluid catalytic cracking">fluid catalytic cracking</a> to convert the longer, more complex molecules in the oil to the shorter, simpler ones in the fuels.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Due to its high <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a>, easy transportability and <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">relative abundance</a>, oil has become the world's most important source of energy since the mid-1950s. Petroleum is also the raw material for many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical" title="Chemical">chemical</a> products, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceuticals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, and plastics; the 16 percent not used for energy production is converted into these other materials. Petroleum is found in <a href="/wiki/Porosity" title="Porosity">porous</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_formations" title="Rock formations">rock formations</a> in the upper <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">strata</a> of some areas of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>. There is also petroleum in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tar_sands" title="Tar sands">oil sands (tar sands)</a>. Known <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">oil reserves</a> are typically estimated at 190 km<sup>3</sup> (1.2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000000_(number)" title="1000000000000 (number)">trillion</a> <a href="/wiki/Long_and_short_scales" title="Long and short scales">(short scale)</a> <a href="/wiki/Barrel_(unit)" title="Barrel (unit)">barrels</a>) without oil sands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> or 595 km<sup>3</sup> (3.74 trillion barrels) with oil sands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Consumption is currently around 84 million barrels (13.4<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup> m<sup>3</sup>) per day, or 4.9 km<sup>3</sup> per year, yielding a remaining oil supply of only about 120 years, if current demand remains static.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> More recent studies, however, put the number at around 50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Octane_molecule_3D_model.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="491" data-file-width="1240" decoding="async" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Octane_molecule_3D_model.png/220px-Octane_molecule_3D_model.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Octane_molecule_3D_model.png/330px-Octane_molecule_3D_model.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Octane_molecule_3D_model.png/440px-Octane_molecule_3D_model.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Octane_molecule_3D_model.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Octane" title="Octane">Octane</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> found in petroleum. Lines represent <a href="/wiki/Single_bond" title="Single bond">single bonds</a>; black spheres represent <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>; white spheres represent <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Petroleum is mainly a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, i.e. containing only carbon and hydrogen. The most common components are <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a> (paraffins), <a href="/wiki/Cycloalkane" title="Cycloalkane">cycloalkanes</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naphthene" title="Naphthene">naphthenes</a>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Aromatic hydrocarbon">aromatic hydrocarbons</a>. They generally have from 5 to 40 carbon atoms per molecule, although trace amounts of shorter or longer molecules may be present in the mixture.
</p><p>The alkanes from <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a> (C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>12</sub>) to <a href="/wiki/Octane" title="Octane">octane</a> (C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>18</sub>) are <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">refined</a> into gasoline, the ones from <a href="/wiki/Nonane" title="Nonane">nonane</a> (C<sub>9</sub>H<sub>20</sub>) to <a href="/wiki/Hexadecane" title="Hexadecane">hexadecane</a> (C<sub>16</sub>H<sub>34</sub>) into <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel fuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jet_fuel" title="Jet fuel">jet fuel</a>. Alkanes with more than 16 carbon atoms can be refined into <a href="/wiki/Fuel_oil" title="Fuel oil">fuel oil</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lubricating_oil" title="Lubricating oil">lubricating oil</a>. At the heavier end of the range, <a href="/wiki/Paraffin_wax" title="Paraffin wax">paraffin wax</a> is an alkane with approximately 25 carbon atoms, while <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a> has 35 and up, although these are usually <a href="/wiki/Fluid_catalytic_cracking" title="Fluid catalytic cracking">cracked</a> by modern refineries into more valuable products. The shortest molecules, those with four or fewer carbon atoms, are in a gaseous state at room temperature. They are the petroleum gases. Depending on demand and the cost of recovery, these gases are either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flare_stack" title="Flare stack">flared off</a>, sold as <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a> under pressure, or used to power the refinery's own burners. During the winter, butane (C<sub>4</sub>H<sub>10</sub>), is blended into the gasoline pool at high rates, because its high vapour pressure assists with cold starts. Liquified under pressure slightly above atmospheric, it is best known for powering cigarette lighters, but it is also a main fuel source for many developing countries. Propane can be liquified under modest pressure, and is consumed for just about every application relying on petroleum for energy, from cooking to heating to transportation.
</p><p>The <i>aromatic hydrocarbons</i> are <a href="/wiki/Degree_of_unsaturation" title="Degree of unsaturation">unsaturated hydrocarbons</a> which have one or more planar six-carbon rings called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benzene_ring" title="Benzene ring">benzene rings</a>, to which hydrogen atoms are attached with the formula C<sub>n</sub>H<sub>2n-6</sub>. They tend to burn with a sooty flame, and many have a sweet aroma. Some are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carcinogenic" title="Carcinogenic">carcinogenic</a>.
</p><p>These different molecules are separated by <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a> at an oil refinery to produce gasoline, jet fuel, kerosene, and other hydrocarbons. For example, <a href="/wiki/2,2,4-Trimethylpentane" title="2,2,4-Trimethylpentane">2,2,4-trimethylpentane</a> (isooctane), widely used in <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, has a chemical formula of C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>18</sub> and it reacts with oxygen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermically</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">18</sub></span></span><sub>(<i>l</i>)</sub> + 25 <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span><sub>(<i>g</i>)</sub> → 16 <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span><sub>(<i>g</i>)</sub> + 18 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span><sub>(<i>g</i>)</sub> (ΔH = −5.51 MJ/mol of octane)</dd></dl>
<p>The number of various molecules in an oil sample can be determined by laboratory analysis. The molecules are typically extracted in a <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a>, then separated in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_chromatograph" title="Gas chromatograph">gas chromatograph</a>, and finally determined with a suitable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detector" title="Detector">detector</a>, such as a <a href="/wiki/Flame_ionization_detector" title="Flame ionization detector">flame ionization detector</a> or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometer" title="Mass spectrometer">mass spectrometer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Due to the large number of co-eluted hydrocarbons within oil, many cannot be resolved by traditional gas chromatography and typically appear as a hump in the chromatogram. This <a href="/wiki/Unresolved_complex_mixture" title="Unresolved complex mixture">Unresolved Complex Mixture</a> (UCM) of hydrocarbons is particularly apparent when analysing weathered oils and extracts from tissues of organisms exposed to oil. Some of the components of oil will mix with water: the <a href="/wiki/Water_associated_fraction" title="Water associated fraction">water associated fraction</a> of the oil.
</p><p>Incomplete combustion of petroleum or gasoline results in production of toxic byproducts. Too little oxygen during combustion results in the formation of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>. Due to the high temperatures and high pressures involved, exhaust gases from gasoline combustion in car engines usually include <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxides</a> which are responsible for creation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photochemical_smog" title="Photochemical smog">photochemical smog</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_petroleum">Fossil petroleum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fossil petroleum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="977" data-file-width="1769" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png/300px-Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png/450px-Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png/600px-Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Treibs%26Chlorophyll.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of a vanadium <a href="/wiki/Porphyrin" title="Porphyrin">porphyrin</a> compound (left) extracted from petroleum by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_E._Treibs" title="Alfred E. Treibs">Alfred E. Treibs</a>, father of <a href="/wiki/Organic_geochemistry" title="Organic geochemistry">organic geochemistry</a>. Treibs noted the close structural similarity of this molecule and <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll_a" title="Chlorophyll a">chlorophyll a</a> (right).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Petroleum is a <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> derived from ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossilized" title="Fossilized">fossilized</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_material" title="Organic material">organic materials</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">[76]</a></sup> Vast amounts of these remains settled to sea or lake bottoms where they were covered in <a href="/wiki/Water_stagnation" title="Water stagnation">stagnant water</a> (water with no dissolved <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Mud" title="Mud">mud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a> faster than they could <a href="/wiki/Decomposition#Anaerobic_vs_aerobic" title="Decomposition">decompose aerobically</a>. Approximately 1 <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">m</a> below this sediment, water oxygen concentration was low, below 0.1 mg/L, and <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_waters" title="Anoxic waters">anoxic conditions</a> existed. Temperatures also remained constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>As further layers settled to the sea or lake bed, intense heat and pressure built up in the lower regions. This process caused the organic matter to change, first into a waxy material known as <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a>, found in various <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">oil shales</a> around the world, and then with more heat into liquid and gaseous <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> via a process known as <a href="/wiki/Catagenesis_(geology)" title="Catagenesis (geology)">catagenesis</a>. Formation of petroleum occurs from hydrocarbon <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> in a variety of mainly <a href="/wiki/Endothermic_process" title="Endothermic process">endothermic</a> reactions at high temperature or pressure, or both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-77">[77]</a></sup> These phases are described in detail below.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anaerobic_decay">Anaerobic decay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Anaerobic decay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the absence of plentiful oxygen, <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism"><i>aerobic</i> bacteria</a> were prevented from decaying the organic matter after it was buried under a layer of sediment or water. However, <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism"><i>anaerobic</i> bacteria</a> were able to reduce <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrates</a> among the matter to <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">H<sub>2</sub>S</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">N<sub>2</sub></a> respectively by using the matter as a source for other reactants. Due to such anaerobic bacteria, at first this matter began to break apart mostly via <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a>: <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> were hydrolyzed to <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">simple sugars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> respectively. These were further anaerobically <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidized</a> at an accelerated rate by the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> of the bacteria: e.g., amino acids went through <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_deamination" title="Oxidative deamination">oxidative deamination</a> to <a href="/wiki/Imino_acid" title="Imino acid">imino acids</a>, which in turn reacted further to <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keto_acid" title="Keto acid">α-keto acids</a>. <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">Monosaccharides</a> in turn ultimately decayed to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. The anaerobic decay products of amino acids, monosaccharides, <a href="/wiki/Phenols" title="Phenols">phenols</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a> combined to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fulvic_acid" title="Fulvic acid">fulvic acids</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">Fats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">waxes</a> were not extensively hydrolyzed under these mild conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kerogen_formation">Kerogen formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Kerogen formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some phenolic compounds produced from previous reactions worked as <a href="/wiki/Bactericide" title="Bactericide">bactericides</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Actinomycetales" title="Actinomycetales">actinomycetales</a> order of bacteria  also produced antibiotic compounds (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Streptomycin" title="Streptomycin">streptomycin</a>). Thus the action of anaerobic bacteria ceased at about 10 m below the water or sediment. The mixture at this depth contained fulvic acids, unreacted and partially reacted fats and waxes, slightly modified <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a>, resins and other hydrocarbons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_76-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">[76]</a></sup> As more layers of organic matter settled to the sea or lake bed, intense heat and pressure built up in the lower regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-77">[77]</a></sup> As a consequence, compounds of this mixture began to combine in poorly understood ways to <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a>. Combination happened in a similar fashion as <a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">phenol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> molecules react to <a href="/wiki/Urea-formaldehyde" title="Urea-formaldehyde">urea-formaldehyde resins</a>, but kerogen formation occurred in a more complex manner due to a bigger variety of reactants. The total process of kerogen formation from the beginning of anaerobic decay is called <b>diagenesis</b>, a word that means a transformation of materials by dissolution and recombination of their constituents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_76-5"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transformation_of_kerogen_into_fossil_fuels">Transformation of kerogen into fossil fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Transformation of kerogen into fossil fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Kerogen formation continued to the depth of about 1 <a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a> from the Earth's surface where temperatures may reach around 50 <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a>. Kerogen formation represents a halfway point between organic matter and fossil fuels: kerogen can be exposed to oxygen, oxidize and thus be lost, or it could be buried deeper inside the <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">Earth's crust</a> and be subjected to conditions which allow it to slowly transform into fossil fuels like petroleum. The latter happened through <b>catagenesis</b> in which the reactions were mostly <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radical</a> <a href="/wiki/Rearrangement_reaction" title="Rearrangement reaction">rearrangements</a> of kerogen. These reactions took thousands to millions of years and no external reactants were involved. Due to radical nature of these reactions, kerogen reacted towards two classes of products: those with low H/C ratio (<a href="/wiki/Anthracene" title="Anthracene">anthracene</a> or products similar to it) and those with high H/C ratio (<a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> or products similar to it); i.e., carbon-rich or hydrogen-rich products. Because catagenesis was closed off from external reactants, the resulting composition of the fuel mixture was dependent on the composition of the kerogen via reaction <a href="/wiki/Stoichiometry" title="Stoichiometry">stoichiometry</a>. Three types of kerogen exist: type I (algal), II (liptinic) and III (humic), which were formed mainly from <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Woody_plant" title="Woody plant">woody plants</a> (this term includes <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">trees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrub" title="Shrub">shrubs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liana" title="Liana">lianas</a>) respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_76-6"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Catagenesis was <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolytic</a> despite the fact that it happened at relatively low temperatures (when compared to commercial pyrolysis plants) of 60 to several hundred °C. Pyrolysis was possible because of the long reaction times involved. Heat for catagenesis came from the decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a> materials of the crust, especially <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40"><sup>40</sup>K</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thorium-232" title="Thorium-232"><sup>232</sup>Th</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235"><sup>235</sup>U</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238"><sup>238</sup>U</a>. The heat varied with <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_gradient" title="Geothermal gradient">geothermal gradient</a> and was typically 10-30 °C per km of depth from the Earth's surface. Unusual <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> intrusions, however, could have created greater localized heating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_76-7"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Oil_window_.28temperature_range.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_window_(temperature_range)">Oil window (temperature range)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Oil window (temperature range)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Geologists often refer to the temperature range in which oil forms as an <i>"oil window"</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford_Edu_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford_Edu-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_76-8"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">[76]</a></sup> Below the minimum temperature oil remains trapped in the form of kerogen. Above the maximum temperature the oil is converted to natural gas through the process of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_cracking" title="Thermal cracking">thermal cracking</a>. Sometimes, oil formed at extreme depths may migrate and become trapped at a much shallower level. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athabasca_Oil_Sands" title="Athabasca Oil Sands">Athabasca Oil Sands</a> are one example of this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_76-9"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abiogenic_petroleum">Abiogenic petroleum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Abiogenic petroleum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An alternative mechanism to the one described above was proposed by Russian scientists in the mid-1850s, the hypothesis of <a href="/wiki/Abiogenic_petroleum_origin" title="Abiogenic petroleum origin">abiogenic petroleum origin</a> (petroleum formed by inorganic means), but this is contradicted by geological and <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">geochemical</a> evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glasby2006_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-glasby2006-80">[80]</a></sup> Abiogenic sources of oil have been found, but never in commercially profitable amounts. "The controversy isn't over whether abiogenic oil reserves exist," said Larry Nation of the American Association of Petroleum Geologists. "The controversy is over how much they contribute to Earth's overall reserves and how much time and effort geologists should devote to seeking them out."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reservoirs">Reservoirs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Petroleum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Reservoirs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output
